    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The persimmon ( Diospyros oleifera Cheng) genome provides new insights into the inheritance of astringency and ancestral evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2019 6:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-12-18"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Persimmon (Diospyros kaki) is an oriental perennial woody fruit tree whose popular fruit is produced and consumed worldwide. The persimmon fruit is unique because of the hyperaccumulation of proanthocyanidins during fruit development, causing the mature fruit of most cultivars to have an astringent taste. In this study, we obtained a chromosome-scale genome assembly for &#8216;Youshi&#8217; (Diospyros oleifera, 2n&#8201;=&#8201;2x&#8201;=&#8201;30), the diploid species of persimmon, by integrating Illumina sequencing, single-molecule real-time sequencing, and high-throughput chromosome conformation capture techniques. The assembled D. oleifera genome consisted of 849.53&#8201;Mb, 94.14% (799.71&#8201;Mb) of which was assigned to 15 pseudochromosomes, and is the first assembled genome for any member of the Ebenaceae. Comparative genomic analysis revealed that the D. oleifera genome underwent an ancient &#947; whole-genome duplication event. We studied the potential genetic basis for astringency development (proanthocyanidin biosynthesis) and removal (proanthocyanidin insolublization). Proanthocyanidin biosynthesis genes were mainly distributed on chromosome 1, and the clustering of these genes is responsible for the genetic stability of astringency heredity. Genome-based RNA-seq identified deastringency genes, and promoter analysis showed that most of their promoters contained large numbers of low oxygen-responsive motifs, which is consistent with the efficient industrial application of high CO2 treatment to remove astringency. Using the D. oleifera genome as the reference, SLAF-seq indicated that &#8216;Youshi&#8217; is one of the ancestors of the cultivated persimmon (2n&#8201;=&#8201;6x&#8201;=&#8201;90). Our study provides significant insights into the genetic basis of persimmon evolution and the development and removal astringency, and it will facilitate the improvement of the breeding of persimmon fruit."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Persimmon (<i>Diospyros kaki</i>) is an oriental perennial woody fruit tree whose popular fruit is produced and consumed worldwide. The persimmon fruit is unique because of the hyperaccumulation of proanthocyanidins during fruit development, causing the mature fruit of most cultivars to have an astringent taste. In this study, we obtained a chromosome-scale genome assembly for ‘Youshi’ (<i>Diospyros oleifera</i>, 2n = 2x = 30), the diploid species of persimmon, by integrating Illumina sequencing, single-molecule real-time sequencing, and high-throughput chromosome conformation capture techniques. The assembled <i>D. oleifera</i> genome consisted of 849.53 Mb, 94.14% (799.71 Mb) of which was assigned to 15 pseudochromosomes, and is the first assembled genome for any member of the Ebenaceae. Comparative genomic analysis revealed that the <i>D. oleifera</i> genome underwent an ancient γ whole-genome duplication event. We studied the potential genetic basis for astringency development (proanthocyanidin biosynthesis) and removal (proanthocyanidin insolublization). Proanthocyanidin biosynthesis genes were mainly distributed on chromosome 1, and the clustering of these genes is responsible for the genetic stability of astringency heredity. Genome-based RNA-seq identified deastringency genes, and promoter analysis showed that most of their promoters contained large numbers of low oxygen-responsive motifs, which is consistent with the efficient industrial application of high CO<sub>2</sub> treatment to remove astringency. Using the <i>D. oleifera</i> genome as the reference, SLAF-seq indicated that ‘Youshi’ is one of the ancestors of the cultivated persimmon (2n = 6x = 90). Our study provides significant insights into the genetic basis of persimmon evolution and the development and removal astringency, and it will facilitate the improvement of the breeding of persimmon fruit.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

            <span>Download PDF</span>
            <svg aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xlink:href="#global-icon-download"/></svg>
        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>Diospyros</i> L. belongs to the Ebenaceae, a plant genus that includes over 500 species that are distributed worldwide<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Duangjai, S., Wallnöfer, B., Samuel, R., Munzinger, J. &amp; Chase, M. W. Generic delimitation and relationships in Ebenaceae sensu lato: evidence from six plastid DNA regions. Am. J. Bot. 93, 1808–1827 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d54987e544">1</a></sup> and is one of the largest angiosperm genera<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Frodin, D. G. History and concepts of big plant genera. Taxon 53, 753–776 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d54987e548">2</a></sup>. Some of its species (e.g., <i>Diospyros kaki</i> Thunb) produce edible fruit, and others are sources of ebony wood, stock, medicinal materials or ornamental plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wang, R. Z., Yang, Y. &amp; Li, G. C. Chinese persimmon germplasm resources. Acta Hortic. 436, 43–50 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d54987e555">3</a></sup>. The most well-known species is <i>Diospyros kaki</i>, which originated from China<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kanzaki, S. The origin and cultivar development of Japanese Persimmon (Diospyros kaki Thunb.). J. Jpn. Soc. Food Sci. Technol. 63, 328–330 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d54987e563">4</a></sup>. One fossil leaf of wild persimmon (<i>Diospyros miokaki</i>) that is ~25 million years old was found in the Shandong Province of China and stored in the Shanwang Paleontological Museum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wu, Z. X. The Shanwang fossils with the passage of time. Openings 7, 74–79 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d54987e570">5</a></sup>. In ancient China, persimmon was first described in ‘Li Ji·Nei Ze’ (<i>circa</i> 450 BC, written by Ji Kong, grandson of Confucius), and it began to be considered an important food during the Warring States Period (475–221 B.C.)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Xin, S. Chinese Fruit History Research. pp 142–143 (Agricultural Publishing House: Beijing, 1983)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d54987e577">6</a></sup>. The persimmon was widely cultivated in China during the Tang Dynasty (618–907 A.D.) due to the development of grafting and technological approaches for removing astringency, and it was introduced to Japan and spread to the Korean peninsula in the 15th century and to Europe and then America in the 18th–20th centuries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wang, R. Z., Yang, Y. &amp; Li, G. C. Chinese persimmon germplasm resources. Acta Hortic. 436, 43–50 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d54987e581">3</a></sup>. Persimmon has been cultivated mainly in oriental countries (e.g., China, Japan, and Korea) but is widely distributed in other areas (e.g., China, Japan, and Korea). Australia, Brazil, Israel, Spain, and the United States)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yonemori, K., Sugiura, A. &amp; Yamada, M. Persimmon genetics and breeding. Plant Breed. Rev. 19, 191–225 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d54987e586">7</a></sup>. The most recent statistical data indicate that persimmon fruit production in China has reached 400 million tons annually (FAO, 2016).</p><p>Persimmon fruit are unique in their accumulation of proanthocyanidins (PAs, also known as condensed tannins, CTs), and soluble CTs (SCTs) cause fruit astringency in most cultivars, even at commercial maturity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Yamada, M., Yamane, H., Sato, A., Hirakawa, N. &amp; Wang, R. Z. Variations in fruit ripening time, fruit weight and soluble solids content of oriental persimmon cultivars native to Japan. J. Jpn. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 63, 485–491 (1994)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d54987e593">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Min, T. et al. Ethylene-responsive transcription factors interact with promoters of ADH and PDC involved in persimmon (Diospyros kaki) fruit de- astringency. J. Exp. Bot. 63, 6393–6405 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d54987e596">9</a></sup>, reducing consumer appeal. Thus, persimmon is a characteristic material for studying PA synthesis, accumulation and metabolism in fruit. PAs also prevent benefits such as strong oxidation resistance, and they are widely used in cosmetics as antioxidants and anti-aging ingredients<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Nakajima, A. &amp; Sakaguchi, T. Uptake and recovery of gold by immobilized persimmon tannin. J. Chem. Technol. Biotechnol. 57, 321–326 (1993)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d54987e600">10</a></sup>. PAs are also widely used in wastewater treatment because of their scavenging ability towards radioactive and heavy metals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nakajima, A. &amp; Sakaguchi, T. Uptake and recovery of gold by immobilized persimmon tannin. J. Chem. Technol. Biotechnol. 57, 321–326 (1993)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d54987e604">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sakaguchi, T. &amp; Nakajima, A. Accumulation of uranium by immobilized persimmon tannin. Sep. Sci. Technol. 29, 205–221 (1994)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d54987e604_1">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nakajima, A. &amp; Baba, Y. Mechanism of hexavalent chromium adsorption by persimmon tannin gel. Water Res. 38, 2859–2864 (2004)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d54987e604_2">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xiong, Y. et al. Selective recovery of precious metals by persimmon waste chemically modified with dimethylamine. Bioresour. Technol. 100, 4083–4089 (2009)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d54987e604_3">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Inoue, K. et al. Hydrometallurgical recovery of precious metals and removal of hazardous metals using persimmon tannin and persimmon wastes. Metals 5, 1921–1956 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d54987e607">14</a></sup>. The biosynthesis of PAs is controlled by a series of structural genes in the shikimate pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Akagi, T. et al. DkMyb4 is a Myb transcription factor Involved in proanthocyanidin biosynthesis in persimmon fruit. Plant Physiol. 151, 2028–2045 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d54987e611">15</a></sup>; however, the molecular mechanisms and evolution of PA accumulation in persimmon remain elusive<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Akagi, T., Ikegami, A. &amp; Yonemori, K. Proanthocyanidin biosynthesis of persimmon (Diospyros kaki Thunb.) fruit. Sci. Hortic. 130, 373–380 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d54987e615">16</a></sup>. In addition to the study of PA metabolism, in recent years, persimmon has been used as a model to understand fruit anaerobic environment interactions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zhu, Q. G. et al. A transcription factor network responsive to high CO2/hypoxia is involved in deastringency in persimmon fruit. J. Exp. Bot. 69, 2061–2070 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d54987e620">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zhu, Q. G. et al. High-CO2/hypoxia-responsive transcription factors DkERF24 and DkWRKY1 interact and activate DkPDC2 promoter. Plant Physiol. 180, 6211–6633 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d54987e623">18</a></sup> and the evolution of sex chromosome systems in higher plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Akagi, T., Henry, I. M., Tao, R. &amp; Comai, L. A Y-chromosome-encoded small RNA acts as a sex determinant in persimmons. Science 346, 646–650 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d54987e627">19</a></sup>. However, most of these studies were conducted based on the de novo assembly of transcriptomic sequences due to the absence of complete persimmon genome sequences for either <i>Diospyros</i> species or other Ebenaceae. This deficiency has inhibited research on evolution and heredity in persimmon and other plants in the Ebenales.</p><p>Within <i>Diospyros</i>, there are three main species cultivated for fruit production: <i>Diospyros lotus</i>, <i>Diospyros oleifera</i>, and <i>Diospyros kaki</i>, and all three of which produce astringent fruit. <i>D. kaki</i> is hexaploid (2n = 6x = 90), while <i>D. lotus</i> and <i>D. oleifera</i> are diploid (2n = 2x = 30)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Guo, D. L. &amp; Luo, Z. R. Genetic relationships of the Japanese persimmon Diospyros kaki (Ebenaceae) and related species revealed by SSR analysis. Genet. Mol. Res. 10, 1060–1068 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d54987e659">20</a></sup>. <i>D. kaki</i> accounts for the greatest number of commercial fruit tree cultivars, and <i>D. lotus</i> is widely used as a rootstock. Compared to <i>D. kaki</i> and <i>D. lotus</i>, <i>D. oleifera</i> exhibits two additional characteristics: the generation of trichomes and the excretion of oils on the fruit surface (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">1</a>). Here, the material ‘Youshi’ (the traditional common name in China for <i>D. oleifera</i> Cheng) was used; Youshi means ‘oil persimmon’ in Chinese. Another Ebenales plant, avocado (<i>Persea americana</i>), is also well known for containing high levels of lipids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Tan, C. X. Virgin avocado oil: an emerging source of functional fruit oil. J. Funct. Foods 54, 381–392 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d54987e688">21</a></sup>. Fu et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Fu, J. M. et al. Five complete chloroplast genome sequences from Diospyros: genome organization and comparative analysis. PLoS ONE 11, e0159566 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d54987e693">22</a></sup> sequenced the complete chloroplast genomes from <i>D. kaki</i>, <i>D. lotus</i>, <i>D. oleifera</i>, <i>D. glaucifolia</i> and <i>D</i>. ‘Jinzaoshi’ and found that <i>D. kaki</i> was more closely related to <i>D. oleifera</i>, whereas <i>D. lotus</i> exhibited a closer relationship with <i>D. glaucifolia</i>. Kanzaki<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kanzaki, S. The origin and cultivar development of Japanese Persimmon (Diospyros kaki Thunb.). J. Jpn. Soc. Food Sci. Technol. 63, 328–330 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d54987e725">4</a></sup> proposed that the commercialized persimmon (<i>D. Kaki</i>) originated from southern China through polyploidization from diploid ancestors. The complete genome sequence for <i>Diospyros</i> would be tremendously useful for clarifying these issues.</p><p>In this study, <i>D. oleifera</i> (‘Youshi’) was chosen as the material for genome sequencing. The PacBio RSII and HiSeq sequencing techniques were used to generate a high-quality genome assembly and annotation for this diploid persimmon. Due to the importance of astringency for the persimmon industry, the genetic basis for astringency development and removal was also investigated. Based on the obtained genome sequence, the first genetic linkage map was constructed for persimmon using SLAF-seq, which provided information about the evolution of persimmon.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>The ‘Youshi’ genome was sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq (Illumina, USA) and PacBio Sequel platforms, and the assembled scaffolds were ordered using the Hi-C technique. The detailed assembly process is illustrated in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">2</a>. The project generated ~86 gigabases (Gb) of high-quality sequences (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">2</a>), representing ~100× coverage of this diploid ‘Youshi’ persimmon genome. The final genome assembly was 849.53 Mb in size, very close to the predicted size of 853.3 Mb based on nuclear weight measurements performed via flow cytometry (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">3</a>), and it consisted of 4728 scaffolds (≥1 kb) with an N50 of 42.43 Mb and 5919 contigs (≥1 kb) with an N50 of 890.84 kb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">3</a>). The GC content of the assembled persimmon genome was 37.40% (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Tab1">1</a>). Notably, 94.14% (799.71 Mb) of the genome was anchored to 15 pseudochromosomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">4</a>), and a total of 556.36 Mb (64.96%, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">5</a>) of repetitive sequences were identified.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table. 1 Statistics of persimmon genome assembly and annotation.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The quality of the assembly was further assessed by three independent methods. First, it was verified that it contained a majority of the core eukaryotic genes (98.03% and 77.02%, respectively, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">6</a>) and genes in the BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs) datasets<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d54987e880">23</a></sup> (89.86%, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">7</a>). Second, the high-throughput sequencing (Illumina HiSeq) data were aligned to the genome sequence using BWA software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d54987e887">24</a></sup><sup>,</sup> and the results indicated that more than 98.58% of the sequences could be mapped to the assembled genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">8</a>). Third, a Hi-C intrachromosomal contact map suggested that all bins could be allocated to 15 pseudochromosomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">4</a>). Thus, all of these results support the conclusion that this assembled ‘Youshi’ persimmon genome is of high quality at the chromosome scale.</p><p>A total of 32,516 putative genes were predicted, with an average gene length of 6773 bp; 80.53% of these genes shared homology with known genes, and 95.95% of these genes were functionally annotated (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">10</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">5</a>). Among these predicted genes, only 25,379 (78.05%) could be anchored to the 15 pseudochromosomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">6</a>). A total of 1540 noncoding RNAs, including miRNAs, rRNAs, and tRNAs, were identified by in silico prediction (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">11</a>). In addition, 4381 pseudogenes were predicted in the persimmon genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">12</a>). Motif and domain annotation analysis based on the amino acid sequences of 32,516 predicted genes indicated a total of 2802 motifs and 36,198 domains (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">13</a>). There were 137 gene syntenic blocks and 3612 paralogous gene groups identified based on the self-alignment of the 25,379 chromosome-anchored genes, indicating that the persimmon genome has undergone frequent interchromosome fusions and segmental duplications during its evolutionary history (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Homologous genome blocks in persimmon."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Homologous genome blocks in persimmon.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0227-2/MediaObjects/41438_2019_227_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0227-2/MediaObjects/41438_2019_227_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="683" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Chr1-15 are pseudochromosomes. The first (green) circle shows the gene density on chromosomes, and the second (purple) circle shows the pseudogene density. The brown, purple and blue dots show the distribution of tRNAs, miRNAs, and rRNAs, respectively. The inner lines represent the relationships between syntenic blocks. Each line represents a syntenic block.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Comparative genomic and genome evolutionary analysis</h3><p>A gene family cluster analysis of the complete gene sets of persimmon (<i>D. oleifera</i>), apple (<i>M. domestica</i>), Arabidopsis (<i>A. thaliana</i>) and grape (<i>V. vinifera</i>) was performed. A total of 25,199 gene families in the persimmon genome were grouped into 13,406 gene clusters, with 7567 gene clusters being shared by all four species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig2">2a</a>). Furthermore, 3644 persimmon-specific genes in 1251 clusters were identified (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">14</a>), which are annotated in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">15</a>. An expanded analysis was performed with 13 other sequenced plant genomes, and single-copy genes were used for phylogenetic tree construction. The results showed that <i>D. oleifera</i> is relatively closely related to <i>Actinidia chinensis</i> (kiwifruit) and <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (tomato) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig2">2b</a>). The phylogenetic tree also indicated that <i>D. oleifera</i> diverged phylogenetically from <i>A. chinensis</i> approximately 77.80 million years ago (Mya), after the divergence of <i>S. lycopersicum</i> at 104.47 Mya (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig2">2b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Comparative genomic analysis of persimmon and other species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Comparative genomic analysis of persimmon and other species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0227-2/MediaObjects/41438_2019_227_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0227-2/MediaObjects/41438_2019_227_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="430" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Venn diagram of shared orthologous gene families in persimmon, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Malus domestica</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>. The number of gene families is listed for each component. <b>b</b> Phylogenetic tree constructed from persimmon single-copy gene families with 13 other species, including <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (Vvinifera_145_Genoscope), <i>Actinidia chinensis</i> (v1.0), <i>Musa acuminata</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (SL2.40), <i>Diospyros oleifera</i>, <i>Citrus sinensis</i>, <i>Glycine max</i> (Soybean_Williams82), <i>Carica papaya</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (TAIR10), <i>Juglans regia</i>, <i>Fragaria vesca</i>, <i>Prunus persica</i> (v2.0), <i>Pyrus bretschneideri</i>, and <i>Malus domestica</i> (v1.0). The numbers indicate the divergence time. <b>c</b> Distribution of 4DTv distance between syntenic orthologous genes. The red arrow indicates the peak value for persimmon. <b>d</b> Gene family expansion and contraction analysis. Gene family expansions and contractions are indicated by numbers in red and blue, respectively. The gray areas represent the conserved gene families.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The 4DTv (fourfold synonymous third-codon transversion) value peaked at ~1.51 for <i>D. oleifera</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig2">2c</a>), indicating that only one ancient γ whole-genome duplication (WGD) occurred in the <i>D. oleifera</i> genome lineage, which was further confirmed by ks analysis (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">7</a>). There were 328 and 493 gene families (annotations are listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">16</a>) showing expansion or contraction, respectively, after divergence from kiwifruit (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig2">2d</a>), suggesting that more <i>D. oleifera</i> gene families have experienced contraction than expansion during adaptive evolution.</p><p>A total of 414 and 3637 genes in the expanded families were annotated (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">17</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">18</a>) to kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes (KEGG) pathways and gene ontology (GO) terms, respectively. KEGG analysis showed that most of the expanded genes were involved in plant-pathogen interactions, starch and sucrose metabolism and phenylpropanoid biosynthesis. GO analysis revealed that in addition to primary metabolic processes, the expanded orthogroups were involved in the defense response, sexual reproduction and flavonoid biosynthetic processes. Forty-seven of the genes from contracted gene families were clustered in 28 KEGG pathways, including plant hormone signal transduction, sesquiterpenoid, and triterpenoid biosynthesis and beta-alanine metabolism (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">19</a>). The GO terms of the genes from contracted gene families were mainly related to oxidation-reduction processes, protein phosphorylation and pentacyclic triterpenoid biosynthetic processes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">20</a>). The functional annotations of the genes in expanded and contracted gene families highlighted various traits of persimmon, including its high contents of sugar and flavonoids, dioecy, low levels of terpenes, and strong adaptability.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Characterization of putative genes in proanthocyanidin biosynthesis pathways</h3><p>A unique feature of persimmon is that it accumulates a high content of PAs during fruit development. In the genome, we found 57 genes involved in PA biosynthesis (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">20</a>), all of which were mapped to the <i>D. oleifera</i> genome. The results indicated that 33.96% of the genes involved in PA biosynthesis were located on chromosome 1 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig3">3</a>) and formed a gene cluster enriched in these genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0227-2/MediaObjects/41438_2019_227_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0227-2/MediaObjects/41438_2019_227_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="247" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Genome location of proanthocyanidin biosynthesis genes in the persimmon genome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genome-wide RNA-seq analysis of astringency removal in persimmon fruit by artificial high CO<sub>2</sub> treatment</h3><p>Most commercialized persimmon cultivars are of the astringent type; thus, understanding PA biosynthesis (astringency formation) and removal (deastringency) is important. A few candidate genes related to alcoholic fermentation and transcription factors that regulate their expression have been previously identified and shown to contribute to astringency removal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Min, T. et al. Ethylene-responsive transcription factors interact with promoters of ADH and PDC involved in persimmon (Diospyros kaki) fruit de- astringency. J. Exp. Bot. 63, 6393–6405 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1142">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zhu, Q. G. et al. A transcription factor network responsive to high CO2/hypoxia is involved in deastringency in persimmon fruit. J. Exp. Bot. 69, 2061–2070 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1145">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zhu, Q. G. et al. High-CO2/hypoxia-responsive transcription factors DkERF24 and DkWRKY1 interact and activate DkPDC2 promoter. Plant Physiol. 180, 6211–6633 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1148">18</a></sup>. However, because of the absence of a complete genome sequence, many of the genes potentially involved in astringency removal were not identified.</p><p>High CO<sub>2</sub> treatment (95% CO<sub>2</sub> + 4% N<sub>2</sub> + 1% O<sub>2</sub>) is the most effective method for tannin removal. This treatment results in elevated ADH and PDC activities and triggers acetaldehyde metabolism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Min, T. et al. Ethylene-responsive transcription factors interact with promoters of ADH and PDC involved in persimmon (Diospyros kaki) fruit de- astringency. J. Exp. Bot. 63, 6393–6405 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1163">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zhu, Q. G. et al. High-CO2/hypoxia-responsive transcription factors DkERF24 and DkWRKY1 interact and activate DkPDC2 promoter. Plant Physiol. 180, 6211–6633 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1166">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ikegami, A., Eguchi, S., Kitajima, A., Inoue, K. &amp; Yonemori, K. Identification of genes involved in proanthocyanidin biosynthesis of persimmon (Diospyros kaki) fruit. Plant Sci. 172, 1037–1047 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1169">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Salvador, A. et al. Physiological and structural changes during ripening and deastringency treatment of persimmon fruit cv. ‘Rojo Brillante’. Postharvest Biol. Technol. 46, 181–188 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1172">26</a></sup>. The acetaldehyde that is produced converts the soluble condensed tannins into insoluble condensed tannins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Tanaka, T., Takahashi, R., Kouno, I. &amp; Nonaka, G. Chemical evidence for the de-astringency (insolubilization of tannins) of persimmon fruit. J. Chem. Soc. 20, 3013–3022 (1994)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1177">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Taira, S., Ikeda, K. &amp; Ohkawa, K. Comparison of insolubility of tannins induced by acetaldehyde vapor in fruit of three types of astringent persimmon. J. Jpn. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 48, 684–687 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1180">28</a></sup>, so that they no longer contribute to astringency. Here, 53 astringent-type cultivars of persimmon fruit (listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">22</a>) were collected and subjected to high CO<sub>2</sub> treatment for 24 h. Four representative cultivars, two showing rapid deastringency after treatment (<i>D. kaki</i> cv. Sigoushi, SGS and cv. Luoyangfangtianshengshi, LYFTSS) and two showing slow deastringency (<i>D. kaki</i> cv. Laopige, LPG and cv. Shijiazhuanglianhuashi, SJZLHS), were selected according to quantitative soluble tannin analysis. After high CO<sub>2</sub> treatment, SGS and LYFTSS exhibited lower soluble tannin contents (&lt;0.1 FW%), while LPG and SJZLHS exhibited higher soluble tannin contents (0.92 and 0.66 FW%, respectively) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig4">4b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Persimmon fruit deastringency analysis and WGCNA analysis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Persimmon fruit deastringency analysis and WGCNA analysis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0227-2/MediaObjects/41438_2019_227_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0227-2/MediaObjects/41438_2019_227_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="503" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Tannin printing of CO<sub>2</sub>-treated persimmon fruit at 1 day in storage. The intensity of black reflects the soluble tannin content. The fruit were treated with high CO<sub>2</sub> (95% CO<sub>2</sub>, 4% N<sub>2</sub>, 1% O<sub>2</sub>) and air (control) within airtight containers for 1 day at 20 °C. <b>b</b> Effects of CO<sub>2</sub> treatment on soluble tannin contents in four selected cultivars of persimmon fruit. Different letters represent significant differences (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05). <b>c</b> WGCNA dendrogram indicating the expression of different gene modules in all 36 persimmon samples. <b>d</b> Trait and module relationship analysis. Different colors represent different modules. <b>e</b> Expression patterns of PK family genes in ‘Jingmianshi’ fruit treated with high CO<sub>2</sub> (95% CO<sub>2</sub> + 4% N<sub>2</sub> + 1% O<sub>2</sub>). <b>f</b> Regulatory effects of DkERF18 and DkERF24 on the DkPK1 promoter.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>When these four cultivars were used for RNA-seq, the differentially expressed genes (DEGs) were clustered into 21 coexpression modules (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig4">4c</a>) through WGCNA (weighted gene coexpression network analysis), and tannin contents were correlated with the module eigengenes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig4">4d</a>). Three significantly negative correlation modules (&lt;−0.6) were found (tan, −0.75; brown, −0.77; yellow, −0.64), and the genes within these modules are listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">23</a>. As expected, genome-wide transcriptomic analysis provided a more comprehensive overview of astringency removal. The previously characterized genes related to alcoholic fermentation, <i>DkPDC1</i> (EVM0027273), <i>DkPDC2</i> (EVM0022732), and <i>DkADH1</i> (EVM0007501), were simultaneously clustered in the brown or yellow module. Moreover, a key enzyme that catalyzes the phosphoenolpyruvate (PEP)-to-pyruvate transition under low-oxygen conditions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">9 </a><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Lasanthi-Kudahettige, R. et al. Transcript profiling of the anoxic rice coleoptile. Plant Physiol. 144, 218–231 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1276">29</a></sup>), the pyruvate kinase (PK) gene (EVM0008535, <i>DkPK1</i>), was found to be one of the hub genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">10</a>). The expression in fruit of members of the <i>DkPK</i> family was verified in ‘Jingmianshi’ fruit (<i>D. kaki</i>, astringent type), and the transcript levels of <i>DkPK1</i> were found to be higher than those of any other members of this gene family (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig4">4e</a>). Furthermore, dual-luciferase assays indicated that two previously characterized hypoxia/high-CO<sub>2</sub>-responsive transcription factors (<i>DkERF18</i> and <i>DkERF24</i>) could trans-activate the promoter of <i>DkPK1</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig4">4f</a>).</p><p>To understand the genetic basis of the failure of astringency removal during fruit development in astringent-type persimmons, the putative promoter regions (−2000 bp) of all of the previously characterized hypoxia/high-CO<sub>2</sub>-responsive transcription factors (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">24</a>) and candidate genes included in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">9</a> were obtained (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">12</a>). Motif analysis indicated that anaerobic-responsive elements (GC-rich motif: GCC[C/G]C, GT-rich motif: GGTTT)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Kyozuka, J., Olive, M., Peacock, W. J., Dennis, E. S. &amp; Shimamoto, K. Promoter elements required for developmental expression of the maize Adh1 gene in transgenic rice. Plant Cell 6, 799–810 (1994)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1332">30</a></sup> were present in all of these promoters (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">12</a>). Each of these genes exhibited more than one anaerobic-responsive element within a single promoter (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">12</a>), and these results confirm the genetic and mechanistic basis of the effectiveness of low oxygen (resulting from high CO<sub>2</sub> treatment) in causing astringency removal.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7"><i>D. oleifera</i> genome-based genetic map analysis</h3><p>Two commercial persimmon cultivars, ‘Taishuu’ <i>(Diospyros kaki</i> cv. Taishuu, male) and ‘Luotiantianshi’ (<i>Diospyros kaki</i> cv. Luotiantianshi, female), were used as parents for hybridization. Due to the high rate of abortion in hybrid seeds, even after using the embryo rescue culture technique<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Xu, Y. et al. Optimization of immature embryo rescue technique system for persimmon. J. Biol. 34, 110–115 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1368">31</a></sup>, only 77 F1 progeny were obtained. Then, an SLAF-seq (specific-locus amplified fragment sequencing) library was constructed for the two persimmon parents and their F1 offspring. The evaluation of the <i>D. kaki</i> SLAF DNA library indicated that the efficiency of <i>Rsa</i>I and <i>Hae</i>III digestion was 86.80%. The percentage of the mapping of the paired-end reads in the control (<i>Oryza sativa</i> L. japonica) was 87.92%. The average sequencing depth of the SLAF markers was 20.40-fold (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">25</a>). After filtering, for the F1 population, 20,405 SLAFs with three segregation patterns were used for genetic map construction, and 11,204 markers were used (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">26</a>). After linkage analysis, 11,204 markers were anchored to 45 high-density linkage groups of <i>D. kaki</i>, with a minimum LOD score (MLOD) of 7.0. The final map was 7168.74 cM long, with an average intermarker distance of 0.64 cM (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">27</a>). The largest LG (linkage group) was LG45, which contained 222 markers with a length of 224.67 cM; the smallest was LG35, which contained 118 markers that spanned 56.23 cM (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">27</a>). The percentage of gaps ≤5 cM between adjacent markers of the 45 LGs ranged from 92.74 to 99.35% (average of 97.13%), and the largest gap was 19.77 cM in LG17 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig5">5a</a>). After correcting genotyping errors using SMOOTH algorithms, 1140 segregation distortion markers with <i>P</i> &lt; 0.01 were retained to increase genomic coverage in the final genetic map, which was further evaluated by the haplotype (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">13</a>) and heat maps (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">14</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Genetic map analysis of persimmon."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Genetic map analysis of persimmon.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0227-2/MediaObjects/41438_2019_227_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0227-2/MediaObjects/41438_2019_227_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="572" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> High-density genetic map of <i>D. kaki</i>. <b>b</b> Correlation between the genetic and physical positions of the SLAF markers. <i>D. kaki</i> represents the genetic group, and the <i>D. oleifera</i> genome represents the physical position.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Based on the <i>D. oleifera</i> genome, the colinear relationship between the <i>D. kaki</i> genetic map and the <i>D. oleifera</i> genome was analyzed. The SLAF sequences of the 11,204 mapped markers were aligned to the genomes using BLASTN analysis with an e-value cutoff of 1e−5, revealing that 26.86% (<i>n</i> = 3009) of the markers were assigned to the <i>D. oleifera</i> genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">20</a>). Moreover, 45 LGs were clearly assigned to the 15 chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig5">5b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">28</a>). Different numbers of LGs were assigned to different chromosomes, such as chromosomes 1/3/5/7/9/12/15, each with two LGs, chromosomes 4/8/10/13/14 were each assigned three LGs, and four, five and seven LGs were assigned to chromosomes 11, 2 and 6, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig5">5b</a>). Using the Spearman correlation coefficient approach, the mapped markers (<i>n</i> = 3009) of the <i>D. kaki</i> genetic map were found to show significant collinearity with the <i>D. oleifera</i> genome, and the correlation between the genetic and physical positions of the SLAF markers is shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#Fig5">5b</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>Persimmon is a widely grown woody tree, and its fruit are valued by consumers because of their high concentrations of antioxidant compounds (especially PAs) and edible fiber, and they serve as a primary source of medicines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wang, R. Z., Yang, Y. &amp; Li, G. C. Chinese persimmon germplasm resources. Acta Hortic. 436, 43–50 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1489">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Peyrat, L. et al. Chemical diversity and antiviral potential in the pantropical Diospyros genus. Fitoterapia 112, 9–15 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1492">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Fan, R. Y. et al. Plant tannin immobilized Fe3O4@SiO2 microspheres: a novel and green magnetic bio-sorbent with superior adsorption capacities for gold and palladium. J. Hazard Mater. 364, 780–789 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1495">33</a></sup>. Although persimmon is economically and culturally important in oriental countries, its genome sequence has not been published. Here, using PacBio and Hi-C technology, the genome sequence of persimmon was assembled from diploid ‘Youshi’ (<i>D. oleifera</i>), which provides a basic resource for the future persimmon research and industrial applications. A genome sequence of 849.53 Mb was assembled with a contig N50 of 0.89 M, and 94.14% of the genome was anchored to 15 pseudochromosomes. The quality of the assembly was further assessed by using core genetic integrity assessment and RNA-seq data. There were 32,516 predicted gene locations in the persimmon genome, which is similar to results from other plant species, such as tomato (<i>N</i> = 34,879)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1505">34</a></sup> and pomegranate (<i>N</i> = 30,903)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Yuan, Z. H. et al. (2018). The pomegtanate (Punica granatum L.) genome provides insights into fruit quality and ovule developmental biology. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 1363–1374 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1513">35</a></sup>. A total of 556.36 Mb (64.96%, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">S7</a>) of repeat sequences were identified, which is similar to the amount in sorghum (62%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1520">36</a></sup> and higher than that in apple (42%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus X domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–841 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1524">37</a></sup>, grapevine (41.4%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="The French-Italian Public Consortium for grapevine Genome Characterization. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–468 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1528">38</a></sup> and rice and <i>Arabidopsis</i> (14 and 17%, respectively)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1536">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1539">40</a></sup>. Additionally, a total of 4012 simple sequence repeats were evenly distributed across the pseudochromosomes, which are beneficial for molecular breeding (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">5</a>). The obtained <i>D. oleifera</i> genome not only provides basic information for persimmon research and utilization but also represents the first reported complete genome for Ebenales. Thus, the <i>D. oleifera</i> genome could be further used for better understanding genome evolution and the relationships of plants in Ebenales.</p><p>The phylogenetic tree compiled from the whole-genome analyses showed that <i>D. oleifera</i> diverged from kiwifruit approximately 77.80 Mya, ago and the estimated times of the separation from the lineages with <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> were ~124.20 and 124.63 years ago, respectively. Following the ancient hexaploidization event (γ) shared by core eudicots, including kiwifruit, there were two additional independent WGD events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Huang, S. X. et al. Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nat. Commun. 4, 2640 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1564">41</a></sup>. For <i>D. oleifera</i>, the 4DTv analysis indicated that persimmon underwent an ancient γ WGD event. Whether this WGD event is shared by other members of Ebenales will have to be determined through further analysis when more Ebenales genome sequences become available.</p><p>The KEGG and GO analyses indicated that the expanded gene families are mainly involved in starch and sucrose metabolism, which may have conferred greater attractiveness on the persimmon fruit during evolution. Some other expanded genes were found to participate in phenylpropanoid biosynthesis, flavonoid biosynthesis, and anthocyanin biosynthesis, and all of these pathways, as well as PA biosynthesis, are derived from the shikimate pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Akagi, T. et al. DkMyb4 is a Myb transcription factor Involved in proanthocyanidin biosynthesis in persimmon fruit. Plant Physiol. 151, 2028–2045 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1574">15</a></sup>. The phenylpropanoids are considered to be key mediators involved in plant resistance to biotic and abiotic stress responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Vogt, T. Phenylpropanoid biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 3, 2–20 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1578">42</a></sup>. These expanded gene families are responsible for the accumulation of PAs in persimmon but also cause fruit astringency, which is not desirable to humans and unexpected animal and insect invasions. In recent years, a non-astringent persimmon that originated from spontaneous mutants of astringent persimmon has increased in importance in fruit industry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yonemori, K., Sugiura, A. &amp; Yamada, M. Persimmon genetics and breeding. Plant Breed. Rev. 19, 191–225 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1582">7</a></sup>. Experiments indicate that astringent persimmons are more resistant to cold than non-astringent persimmons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wang, R. Z., Yang, Y. &amp; Li, G. C. Chinese persimmon germplasm resources. Acta Hortic. 436, 43–50 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1586">3</a></sup>. Thus, the expansion of the relevant gene families may have provided an important way to generate abiotic and biotic resistance in persimmon, which would have been critical for the evolution of persimmon and the expansion of its cultivation.</p><p>The characteristics of <i>D. kaki</i> persimmon and ‘Youshi’ (<i>D. oleifera</i>) related to astringency development and removal were selected for further genome-based investigation. The fruit of hexaploid persimmon cultivars such as ‘Youshi’ also exhibit active PA biosynthesis. The basis for this characteristic was investigated via the genome-wide analysis of PA biosynthesis genes. Most of the PA biosynthesis genes were located on chromosome 1, where they formed a gene cluster. In the plant genome, it is common to find highly homologous genes involved in secondary metabolite synthesis located in a cluster on a chromosome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Richard, G. F., Kerrest, A. &amp; Dujon, B. Comparative genomics and molecular dynamics of DNA repeats in Eukaryotes. Microbiol. Mol. Biol. Rev. 72, 686–727 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1599">43</a></sup>. These gene clusters may originate from duplication and divergence, and clustering may also improve gene function. Furthermore, related traits are likely to be stably inherited, and the genes in clusters could be regulated simultaneously. Most metabolites synthesized by gene clusters are defense or tolerance related<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Field, B. &amp; Osbourn, A. E. Metabolic diversification-independent assembly of operon-like gene clusters in different plants. Science 320, 543–547 (2008)." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1603">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rocha, E. P. C. The organization of the bacterial genome. Annu. Rev. Genet. 42, 211–233 (2008)." href="#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1603_1">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Osbourn, A. Secondary metabolic gene clusters: evolutionary toolkits for chemical innovation. Trends Genet. 26, 449–457 (2010)." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1603_2">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Takos, A. M. &amp; Rook, F. Why biosynthetic genes for chemical defense compounds cluster. Trends Plant Sci. 17, 383–388 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1606">47</a></sup>. Arabidopsis, apple, and grape are predicted to exhibit 18, 73, and 23 genes involved in PA biosynthesis, respectively, but these genes are widely distributed on different chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus X domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–841 (2010)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1610">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="The French-Italian Public Consortium for grapevine Genome Characterization. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–468 (2007)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1610_1">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1613">39</a></sup>. Thus, the clustering of PA biosynthesis genes provides the genetic basis for high PA content in persimmon. On the other hand, genome-based RNA-seq provided new insights regarding astringency removal by identifying the previously characterized <i>PDC</i> and <i>ADH</i> genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Min, T. et al. Ethylene-responsive transcription factors interact with promoters of ADH and PDC involved in persimmon (Diospyros kaki) fruit de- astringency. J. Exp. Bot. 63, 6393–6405 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1624">9</a></sup> and new hypoxia-responsive genes (e.g., <i>DkPK1</i> and its transcriptional regulation). In <i>Arabidopsis</i>, a genome-wide investigation in seedlings predicted 49 core hypoxia-responsive genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Mustroph, A. et al. Profiling translatomes of discrete cell population resolves altered cellular priorities during hypoxia in Arabidopsis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 18843–18848 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1634">48</a></sup>. Here, the assembled genome allowed the rapid searching of low-oxygen-responsive motifs in the putative promoter regions of these candidate genes. Our findings suggested that all of the candidate genes exhibited multiple low-oxygen-responsive motifs in their promoters (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM1">11</a>), which provided genetic evidence of their role and effectiveness in postharvest astringency loss in response to a low oxygen environment.</p><p>High-density linkage maps are the basis for QTL fine mapping of traits, map-based gene cloning and marker-assisted breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fan, L. P. et al. A high-density genetic map of extra-long staple cotton (Gossypium barbadense) constructed using genotyping-by-sequencing based single nucleotide polymorphic markers and identification of fiber traits-related QTL in a recombinant inbred line population. BMC Genomics 19, 489 (2018)." href="#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1649">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gao, W. et al. A genetic linkage map of Pleurotus tuoliensis integrated with physical mapping of the de novo sequenced genome and the mating type loci. BMC Genomics 19, 18 (2018)." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1649_1">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Wang, Z. H. et al. Development of a high-density genetic map based on specific length amplified fragment sequencing and its application in quantitative trait loci analysis for yield-related traits in cultivated peanut. Front. Plant Sci. 9, 827 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1652">51</a></sup>. In persimmon, germplasm genetic diversity and marker-trait associations have been explored using different types of molecular markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Badenes, M. et al. Genetic diversity of introduced and local Spanish persimmon cultivars revealed by RAPD markers. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 50, 579–585 (2003)." href="#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1656">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yonemori, K. et al. Relationship of European persimmon (Diospyros kaki Thunb.) cultivars to Asian cultivars, characterized using AFLPs. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 55, 81–89 (2008)." href="#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1656_1">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Du, X. Y., Zhang, Q. L. &amp; Luo, Z. R. Development of retrotransposon primers and their utilization for germplasm identification in Diospyros spp. (Ebenaceae). Tree Genet. Genomes 5, 235–245 (2009)." href="#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1656_2">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kanzaki, S. et al. SCAR markers for practical application of marker-assisted selection in persimmon (Diospyros kaki Thunb.) Breeding. J. Jpn. Soc. Hortic.
