Sci. 79, 150–155 (2010)." href="#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1656_3">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Onoue, N., Kobayashi, S., Kono, A. &amp; Sato, A. SSR-based molecular profiling of 237 persimmon (Diospyros kaki Thunb.) germplasms using an ASTRINGENCY-linked marker. Tree Genet. Genomes 14, 28 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1659">56</a></sup>. However, none of this research has led to the construction of a high-quality genetic map for persimmon or for any member of the <i>Diospyros</i> genus, due to the limited number of hybrid offspring produced because of the abortion of hybrid seeds. SLAF-seq provides an economical and efficient method for the linkage mapping of nonmodel species with complex genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Sun, X. W. et al. SLAF-seq: an efficient method of large-scale de novo SNP discovery and genotyping using high-throughput sequencing. PLoS ONE 8, e58700 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1666">57</a></sup>. Here, we obtained 77 F1 offspring from ‘Taishuu’ × ‘Luotiantianshi’ via the embryo rescue culture technique<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Xu, Y. et al. Optimization of immature embryo rescue technique system for persimmon. J. Biol. 34, 110–115 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1670">31</a></sup> and constructed the first <i>D. kaki</i> genetic linkage map, with 11,204 markers spanning 7,168.74 cM. The average intermarker distance was 0.64 cM, which is comparable to the resolution reported for high-density linkage maps of <i>Vigna unguiculata</i> (0.35 cM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Huang, H. H. et al. High-density genetic map construction and comparative genome analysis in asparagus bean. Sci. Rep. 8, 4836 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1681">58</a></sup>, <i>Arachis hypogaea</i> (0.58 cM;<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Wang, Z. H. et al. Development of a high-density genetic map based on specific length amplified fragment sequencing and its application in quantitative trait loci analysis for yield-related traits in cultivated peanut. Front. Plant Sci. 9, 827 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1688">51</a></sup> 0.7 cM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Gabay, G. et al. High-resolution genetic linkage map of European pear (Pyrus communis) and QTL fine-mapping of vegetative budbreak time. BMC Plant Biol. 18, 175 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1692">59</a></sup>), <i>Pleurotus tuoliensis</i> (1.0 cM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Gao, W. et al. A genetic linkage map of Pleurotus tuoliensis integrated with physical mapping of the de novo sequenced genome and the mating type loci. BMC Genomics 19, 18 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1700">50</a></sup> and <i>Gossypium barbadense</i> (1.09 cM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Fan, L. P. et al. A high-density genetic map of extra-long staple cotton (Gossypium barbadense) constructed using genotyping-by-sequencing based single nucleotide polymorphic markers and identification of fiber traits-related QTL in a recombinant inbred line population. BMC Genomics 19, 489 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1707">49</a></sup>. This genetic map should be useful for persimmon breeding as a genome reference and for establishing marker-trait associations, providing a basis for understanding the persimmon genome and its inheritance. These markers will be useful for screening important biological properties of persimmon, especially related to the accumulation of tannins and other factors affecting taste.</p><p>As the genome of hexaploid cultivated persimmon remains unpublished, the availability of the <i>D. oleifera</i> genome provides an alternative comparable reference for <i>D. kaki</i>. Based on the <i>D. oleifera</i> genome, 26.86% of the 11,204 markers (<i>n</i> = 3009) in the <i>D. kaki</i> genetic linkage map were assigned to and showed high colinearity with the newly completed <i>D. oleifera</i> genome sequences. Thus, it can be proposed that <i>D. oleifera</i> was one of the diploid ancestors of hexaploid <i>D. kaki</i>, and this evidence supports the previous hypothesis of Kanzaki<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kanzaki, S. The origin and cultivar development of Japanese Persimmon (Diospyros kaki Thunb.). J. Jpn. Soc. Food Sci. Technol. 63, 328–330 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1739">4</a></sup> and the preliminary predictions resulting from genomic in situ hybridization (GISH) studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Choi, Y. A., Tao, R., Yonemori, K. &amp; Sugiura, A. Genomic in situ hybridization between persimmon (Diospyros kaki) and several wild species of Diospyros. J. Jpn. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 72, 385–399 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1743">60</a></sup> and the comparison of chloroplast genome sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Fu, J. M. et al. Five complete chloroplast genome sequences from Diospyros: genome organization and comparative analysis. PLoS ONE 11, e0159566 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1747">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Yonemori, K. et al. Sequence analyses of the ITS regions and the matK gene for determining phylogenetic relationships of Diospyros kaki (persimmon) with other wild Diospyros (Ebenaceae) species. Tree Genet. Genomes 4, 149–158 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1750">61</a></sup>. The relationship between chromosomes and LGs suggested that <i>D. oleifera</i> may also be closely related to other ancestors of <i>D. kaki</i>, and chromosome breakage and rearrangement may have occurred during the evolution of <i>D. kaki</i> from <i>D. oleifera</i>.</p><p>In summary, the first genome of Ebenaceae, from <i>D. oleifera</i>, was sequenced and found to be approximately 849.53 Mb in size (N50 &gt; 0.8 M), with 94.14% (799.71 Mb) of the sequences anchored onto 15 pseudochromosomes. Comparative genomics analysis revealed the genetic basis of astringency development (PA synthesis) and removal in persimmon via precipitation, caused by the production of acetaldehyde during the low-oxygen response. Furthermore, the first genetic map of persimmon was constructed and indicated that <i>D. oleifera</i> was one of the ancestors of cultivated persimmon. The persimmon genome represents an invaluable resource for the genetic improvement of the fruit and for better understanding of its genome evolution. Genetic markers can be developed based on this genome sequence, which will help to accelerate persimmon breeding.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genome size estimation</h3><p>Young leaves of <i>D. oleifera</i> from three branches were used as the sample for genome sequencing (with each tree as one repetition). Nuclear DNA was isolated from fresh leaf tissue using a protocol developed by Doležel et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Doležel, J., Greilhuber, J. &amp; Suda, J. Estimation of nuclear DNA content in plants using flow cytometry. Nat. Protoc. 2, 2233–2244 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1791">62</a></sup>, and flow cytometry (BD FACScalibur, BD Biosciences, USA) was applied to estimate the nuclear DNA content. The reference standard was <i>Zea mays</i> ‘B73’ (2.35 pg/1C)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1798">63</a></sup>. The genome size of <i>D. oleifera</i> was calculated as the ratio between the sample and standard peaks multiplied by the genome size of the standard.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">De novo genome assembly and quality assessment</h3><p>Mature leaves of <i>D. oleifera</i> ‘Youshi’ were collected from the National Persimmon Germplasm Resources Nursery (Northwest A&amp;F University, Shaanxi, China), and the CTAB method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Chang, S., Puryear, J. &amp; Cairney, J. A simple and efficient method for isolating RNA from pine trees. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 11, 113–116 (1993)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1816">64</a></sup> was applied to extract the genomic DNA from the leaves. Library construction, sequencing, assembly and quality assessment for the ‘Youshi’ genome were conducted by Biomarker Technologies Corporation (Beijing, China). According to standard Illumina protocols, two paired-end (PE) libraries with insert sizes of 220 and 500 bp and 14 mate-pair (MP) libraries with insert sizes of 3, 4, 5, 8, 10, 15, and 17 kb were constructed. The Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform (Illumina, San Diego, CA, USA) was used to sequence these libraries and produced 88.35 Gb of raw sequencing data, representing 100X genome coverage. Reads were trimmed for adaptor removal and quality enhancement. NxTrim was used to collapse the duplicated reads of the MP libraries. A deduplication step was performed on the MP libraries.</p><p>The genome assembly and contigs were constructed and analyzed by ALLPATHS-LG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Gnerre, S. &amp; Jaffe, D. B. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Nat. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513–1518 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1823">65</a></sup> with default parameters. SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1827">66</a></sup> was used to link the contigs with support from the paired MP reads. Gaps were filled with GapCloser<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1831">67</a></sup>. In preparation for the assembly, scaffolds &lt;1000 bp were removed. PacBio library construction included DNA purification, damage repair, hairpin adaptor ligation, and digestion with exonucleases to remove damaged DNA and fragments without adaptors. The sizes of the templates (≥15 Kb) were selected via Blue Pippin electrophoresis, and the RS II platform (Pacific Biosciences, California, USA) using P6-C4 sequencing chemistry was then applied to sequence the library. The correction of the draft genome assembly was performed with the Canu v1.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1835">68</a></sup>, WTDBG v1.2.8 (available at <a href="https://github.com/ruanjue/wtdbg">https://github.com/ruanjue/wtdbg</a>) and Falcon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Meth. 13, 1050–1056 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1844">69</a></sup> (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">31</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">32</a>) assemblers. Finally, the assembly-merging method was utilized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Chakraborty, M., Baldwin-Brown, J. G., Long, A. D. &amp; Emerson, J. J. Contiguous and accurate de novo assembly of metazoan genomes with modest long read coverage. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, e147 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1854">70</a></sup> to optimize the assembly results.</p><p>Another assembly method for scaffold anchoring using Hi-C sequencing technology was applied. The gDNA was digested with <i>Hind</i>III. The 5′ overhangs were filled with biotinylated nucleotides; free blunt ends were ligated; and the DNA was purified and sheared to 300–700 bp fragments after biotin was removed. PCR was applied to enrich the biotin-tagged fragments, and the DNA was then used to construct an Illumina library, which was on the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform. A total of 55.41 Gb of clean data (65 × the genome size) were obtained, and 62.20 M valid interaction pairs were detected to assist in genome assembly. The evaluation of the alignment efficiency and insert length distribution for valid pair fragments and valid interaction pairs was performed with HiC-Pro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Servant, N. et al. HiC-Pro: an optimized and flexible pipeline for Hi-C data processing. Genome Biol. 16, 259 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1864">71</a></sup>. The genome sequence contigs and scaffolds were divided into subgroups and sorted and oriented into super scaffolds using LACHESIS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119–1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1868">72</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Final assembly and validation</h3><p>To improve the persimmon genome assembly and chromosome anchoring accuracy, the Illumina data, PacBio long reads and Hi-C data were combined and mutually corrected. Finally, the persimmon genome was assembled into 856.41 Mb of scaffold sequences. We found that 99.99% (856.35 Mb) of the genome consisted of the contig sequences. The assembly contained 4728 scaffolds, with a scaffold N50 of 42.42 Mb, composed of 5919 contigs, with a contig N50 of 890.84 kb. The maximum scaffold and contig lengths were 57.59 Mb and 9.38 Mb, respectively.</p><p>The Hi-C contact heatmap was inspected to confirm the accuracy of the Hi-C assembly. To facilitate the formation of Hi-C contacts, the sequences were proximate in three dimensions if they were adjacent to one another in one dimension. The number of Hi-C links between 100-kb windows on the pseudochromosomes of the final assembly is represented by the intensity of red coloration. More pronounced contact frequencies indicate more Hi-C links between the two bins. An elevated link frequency was observed with a diagonal pattern within individual pseudochromosomes, indicating increased interaction contacts between adjacent regions. The second-generation short sequencing reads from the RNAseq data were also used to validate the final assembly. Among the 250,587,312 reads, 98.60% were mapped successfully back to the final persimmon genome, with a 93.78% properly paired mapping rate.</p><p>CEGMA (version 2.5), with 248 conserved core eukaryotic genes, and BUSCO (version 2.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1886">23</a></sup>, with 1440 genes in embryophyta odb9, were used to assess the completeness and accuracy of the assembled persimmon genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Genome annotation</h3><p>A pipeline that integrated de novo gene prediction, homology-based gene models and RNAseq-based gene models was used to predict protein-coding genes. For de novo prediction, the genes in the genome were predicted with four predictors: Genscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of complete gene structures in human genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol. 268, 78–94 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1898">73</a></sup>, Augustus (version 2.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19(Suppl 2), ii215–ii225 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1902">74</a></sup>, GlimmerHMM (version 3.0.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878–2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1906">75</a></sup>, GeneID (version 1.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Blanco, E., Parra, G. &amp; Guigó, R. Using geneid to identify genes. Curr. Protoc. Bioinforma. Chapter 4, Unit 4.3 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1910">76</a></sup> and SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinforma. 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1914">77</a></sup>. For homology prediction, GeMoMa (version 1.3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Keilwagen, J. et al. Using intron position conservation for homology-based gene prediction. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, e89 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1919">78</a></sup> was used, with protein sequences from <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>Z. jujuba</i> and <i>C. sinensis</i> and gene models with a minimum 50% coverage and 50% query/hit coverage.</p><p>Two complementary methods, one de novo based and the other homology based, were used to identify and classify transposable elements (TEs). LTR FINDER (version 1.05)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1938">79</a></sup>, MITE-Hunter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Han, Y. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e199 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1942">80</a></sup>, RepeatScout (version 1.0.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21(Suppl 1), i351–i358 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1946">81</a></sup> and PILER-DF (version 2.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Edgar, R. C. &amp; Myers, E. W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21(Suppl 1), i152–i158 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1950">82</a></sup> were run to construct the de novo repeat libraries. RepeatMasker (version open-4.0.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. S. Using repeatmasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinforma. 25, 4.10.1–4.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1954">83</a></sup> with WUBlast searches against the RepBase database (RepBase 19.06)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1959">84</a></sup> was applied to build the homology-based repeat library. To obtain a consensus library, PASTEClassifer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Hoede, C. et al. PASTEC: an automatic transposable element classification tool. PLoS ONE 9, e91929 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1963">85</a></sup> was used to process and classify the two libraries. To identify the TEs in the persimmon genome, RepeatMasker was employed, using the consensus library to mask the genome; the masked genome was used for gene prediction.</p><p>For RNA-seq prediction, processed reads were aligned to the reference genome using HISAT2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357–360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1970">86</a></sup>. HISAT (version 2.0.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Pertea, M., Kim, D., Pertea, G. M., Leek, J. T. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Transcript-level expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with HISAT, StringTie and Ballgown. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1650–1667 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1974">87</a></sup> and Stringtie (version 1.2.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Pertea, M., Kim, D., Pertea, G. M., Leek, J. T. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Transcript-level expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with HISAT, StringTie and Ballgown. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1650–1667 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1978">87</a></sup> were used to assemble the transcripts. Trinity (version 2.1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Trinity: reconstructing a full-lentgh transcriptome without a genome from RNA-Seq data. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1982">88</a></sup> was applied for the de novo assembly of the RNA-seq reads, and the RNA-seq reads were analyzed with PASA (version 2.0.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Campbell, M. A., Haas, B. J., Hamilton, J. P., Mount, S. M. &amp; Buell, C. R. Comprehensive analysis of alternative splicing in rice and comparative analyses with Arabidopsis. BMC Genomics 7, 327 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1986">89</a></sup> for the prediction of unigenes. TransDecoder (version 2.0) (Haas, <a href="http://transdecoder.github.io">http://transdecoder.github.io</a>) and GeneMarkS-T (version 5.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: robust scaffold ordering based onmultiple maps. Genome Biol. 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1995">90</a></sup> were used to predict open reading frames (ORFs). Finally, an integrated gene set was produced by EVM (version 1.1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d54987e1999">91</a></sup>. The RNA-seq assemblies were employed to determine untranslated regions (UTRs). The longest transcripts for each locus were retained, and regions outside of the ORFs were designated as UTRs.</p><p>The Rfam database (version 12.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Kozomara, A. &amp; Griffithsjones, S. MiRBase: annotating high confidence microRNAs using deep sequencing data. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D68–D73 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2006">92</a></sup> and MirBase database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Griffiths-Jones, S., Grocock, R. J., van Dongen, S., Bateman, A. &amp; Enright, A. J. MiRBase: microRNA sequences, targets and gene nomenclature. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, D140–D144 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2010">93</a></sup> were searched with Infernal (version 1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Nawrocki, E. P. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics 29, 2933–2935 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2014">94</a></sup> to detect microRNAs and rRNAs. tRNAscan-SE (version 1.3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. TRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2018">95</a></sup> with eukaryote parameters was applied to predict transfer RNAs (tRNAs).</p><p>GenBlastA (version 1.0.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Rong, S., Chu, J. S. C., Ke, W., Jian, P. &amp; Chen, N. GenBlastA: enabling BLAST to identify homologous gene sequences. Genome Res. 19, 143–149 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2026">96</a></sup> was used to identify homologous sequences in the genome by using the integrated gene set as the query, and GeneWise (version 2.4.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. Genewise and genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2030">97</a></sup> was used to define pseudogenes containing premature stop codons or frameshift mutations with 60% identity and coverage.</p><p>Protein sequences were compared against several databases, including NR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Marchler-Bauer, A. et al. CDD: a conserved domain database for the functional annotation of proteins. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, D225–D229 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2037">98</a></sup>, KOG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Tatusov, R. L. et al. The COG database: new development in phylogenetic classification of proteins from complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 29, 22–28 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2041">99</a></sup>, KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: Kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27–30 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2045">100</a></sup>, and TrEMBL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 365–370 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2049">101</a></sup>, using BLAST (version 2.2.31)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2053">102</a></sup> with an E-value cutoff of 1E-5 to annotate the functions of the predicted genes. GO annotations were assigned by BLAST2GO (version 2.5);<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674–3676 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2058">103</a></sup> InterProScan (version 5.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Zdobnov, E. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan- an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847–848 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2062">104</a></sup> and Hmmscan (HMMER, version 3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Mistry, J., Finn, R. D., Eddy, S. R., Bateman, A. &amp; Punta, M. Challenges in homology search: HMMER3 and convergent evolution of coiled-coil regions. Nucleic Acids Res. 41, e121 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2066">105</a></sup> were used for searching; and annotatemotifs and domains were annotated by searching the Pfam database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Finn, R. D. et al. Pfam: clans, web tools and services. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, 247–251 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2070">106</a></sup>, PROSITE database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Bairoch, A. PROSITE: a dictionary of sites and patterns in proteins. Nucleic Acids Res. 19, 2241–2245 (1991)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2074">107</a></sup> and HAMAP database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 108" title="Lima, T. et al. HAMAP: a database of completely sequenced microbial proteome sets and manually curated microbial protein families in UniProtKB/Swiss-Prot. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D471–D478 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR108" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2078">108</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Comparative genomic analysis</h3><p>The available protein sequence sets were collected from 14 sequenced plant species: <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (Vvinifera_145_Genoscope), <i>Actinidia chinensis</i> (v1.0), <i>Musa acuminata</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (SL2.40), <i>Diospyros oleifera</i>, <i>Citrus sinensis</i>, <i>Glycine max</i> (Soybean_Williams82), <i>Carica papaya</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (TAIR10), <i>Juglans regia</i>, <i>Fragaria vesca</i>, <i>Prunus persica</i> (v2.0), <i>Pyrus bretschneideri</i>, and <i>Malus domestica</i> (v1.0). OrthoMCL (version 2.0; mcl inflation factor of 1.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 109" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR109" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2135">109</a></sup> was used to identify orthologous genes. Pairwise sequence similarities were calculated by all-against-all BLASTP (Blast + version 2.3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 110" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinforma. 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR110" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2139">110</a></sup> with a <i>P</i>-value cutoff of 1e−5 and a minimum match length of 50%.</p><p>MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2149">111</a></sup> was used to perform the phylogenetic analysis and alignment using the coding sequences of common single-copy genes. The jModelTest output was used to select the protein model, and the evolutionary tree was constructed via the TIM2+I+G model. The divergence time was estimated with MCMCTREE of PAML (version 4.7a)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 112" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR112" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2153">112</a></sup>. Gene family expansion and contraction were analyzed by using CAFÉ (version 4.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 113" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J. &amp; Hahn, M. CAFÉ: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269–1271 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR113" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2157">113</a></sup> with a maximum-likelihood model. The gene family clustering results and estimated divergence times between species were used. The value of the birth and death parameter (λ) was 0.02, and the <i>P</i>-value was 0.01.</p><p>MCscan was applied to detect the 4DTV gene pairs calculated using the HKY substitution model. MCscan with the YN00 program of the PAML package was used to detect the synonymous mutation rate (ks) of gene pairs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Persimmon fruit deastringency treatment and transcriptome sequencing</h3><p>Commercially mature persimmon fruit from 53 astringent types were harvested from the National Persimmon Germplasm Resources Nursery (Northwest A&amp;F University, Shaanxi, China) in 2017. To remove the astringency from mature fruit, high CO<sub>2</sub> (95% CO<sub>2</sub> + 4% N<sub>2</sub> + 1% O<sub>2</sub>, 1 d) treatment was applied as described by Zhu et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zhu, Q. G. et al. A transcription factor network responsive to high CO2/hypoxia is involved in deastringency in persimmon fruit. J. Exp. Bot. 69, 2061–2070 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2183">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zhu, Q. G. et al. High-CO2/hypoxia-responsive transcription factors DkERF24 and DkWRKY1 interact and activate DkPDC2 promoter. Plant Physiol. 180, 6211–6633 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2186">18</a></sup>, and fruit treated in air were used as the control (CK). The treatments were carried out with three biological replicates. At each sampling time, flesh samples were bulked and frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at −80 °C for further use.</p><p>Fruit astringency was evaluated by two different methods. First, soluble tannins were visualized via the tannin printing method according to Min et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 114" title="Min, T. et al. Isolation and expression of NAC genes during persimmon fruit postharvest astringency removal. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 16, 1894–1906 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR114" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2193">114</a></sup>. The fruit were cut lengthwise immediately after treatment and then pressed onto 5% FeCl<sub>2</sub>-soaked filter paper for 5 s. The intensity of the resulting black color after the removal of fruit indicated the soluble tannin content, and the filter paper was photographed. The soluble tannin contents of frozen samples were also quantified with the Folin-Ciocalteau reagent according to the method described by Yin et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 115" title="Yin, X. R. et al. Expression of ethylene response genes during persimmon fruit astringency removal. Planta 235, 895–906 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR115" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2199">115</a></sup>. The results were calculated using a standard curve of tannin acid equivalents g<sup>−1</sup> fresh weight. All measurements were conducted with three biological replicates.</p><p>Four cultivars showing obvious differences in the rates of deastringency (e.g., rapid deastringency, Sigoushi and Luoyangfangtianshengshi; slow deastringency, Laopige, and Shijiazhuanglianhuashi) were selected for RNA-seq analysis. A total of 36 samples (0 d, 1 d for CK and CO<sub>2</sub>-treated fruit) with three biological replicates were used for total RNA extraction via the CTAB protocol described by Chang et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Chang, S., Puryear, J. &amp; Cairney, J. A simple and efficient method for isolating RNA from pine trees. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 11, 113–116 (1993)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2210">64</a></sup>. An Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer (Agilent Technologies, Santa Cara, California, USA), a NanoDrop 2000 spectrophotometer (Thermo Scientific, Waltham, MA, USA), and a Qubit 2.0 fluorometer (ThermoFisher Scientific, Waltham, MA, US) were used to assess RNA integrity, purity, and concentrations, respectively. A TruSeq RNA Sample Preparation Kit was used to prepare RNA-seq libraries following the manufacturer’s instructions, and the libraries were sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform (Illumina, San Diego, CA, USA). Raw reads were trimmed to remove adaptors and increase quality. Reads &lt;100 bp were discarded after trimming. Overall, each sample produced an average of 7.28 Gb clean data.</p><p>The clean reads were mapped to the genome by using HISAT2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357–360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2217">86</a></sup> with the default parameters. StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 116" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290–295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR116" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2221">116</a></sup> was used to assemble the transcripts. The fragments per kilobase of transcript per million fragments mapped (FPKM) values were used to measure gene expression. DEseq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 117" title="Anders, S. &amp; Huber, W. Differential expression analysis for sequence count data. Genome Biol. 11, R106 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR117" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2225">117</a></sup> was applied to identify differentially expressed genes (DEGs). The false discovery rate was used to adjust the <i>P</i>-values. Genes with significant differences in expression (i.e., log<sub>2</sub> foldchange &gt;1 and adjusted <i>P</i>-value &lt; 0.01) were considered DEGs and were annotated with GO terms and KEGG pathways.</p><p>Coexpression networks were constructed using the WGCNA (v1.66) package in R (v3.5.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 118" title="Langfelder, P. &amp; Horvath, S. WGCNA: an R package for weighted correlation network analysis. BMC Bioinfomatics 9, 559 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR118" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2242">118</a></sup>. Genes with expression values (FPKM) in any variable equal to or higher than 1 were selected for WGCNA. The automatic network construction function <i>blockwiseModules ()</i> with the following settings: soft power 16, minModulesSize 30, mergeCutHeight 0.25 and default parameters for other settings, was used to construct the models. The soft power was chosen by the function <i>pickSoftThreshold ()</i> in WGCNA. The correlations between module eigengenes, the first principle of each module, and the content of tannins were calculated with function <i>cor ()</i> in R with default settings. Candidate hub genes in “tan”, “brown” and “yellow” were picked by thresholding at a value of 0.6. The coexpression network of 295 selected genes was visualized with Cytoscape (v3.7.1).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Real-time PCR</h3><p>For real-time PCR, gene-specific oligonucleotide primers were designed, which are described in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">32</a>. Melting curves and product resequencing were used to assess the quality and specificity of each pair of primers. Real-time PCR was carried out on a Bio-Rad CFX96 Real-Time PCR System using SsoFast<sup>TM</sup> EvaGreen Supermix (Bio-Rad, USA) following the manufacturer’s instructions. The housekeeping gene <i>DkACT</i> (GenBank No. AB473616)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Akagi, T. et al. DkMyb4 is a Myb transcription factor Involved in proanthocyanidin biosynthesis in persimmon fruit. Plant Physiol. 151, 2028–2045 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2271">15</a></sup> was chosen as the internal control, and the 2<sup>−ΔCt</sup> method was used to calculate the relative expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 119" title="Livak, K. J. &amp; Schmittgen, T. D. Analysis of relative gene expression data using real-time quantitative PCR and the 2−ΔΔCT method. Methods 25, 402–408 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR119" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2278">119</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Dual-luciferase assay</h3><p>The in vivo regulatory effects of the previously characterized high-CO<sub>2</sub>/hypoxia-responsive transcription factors on the newly identified deastringency-related gene (<i>DkPK1</i>) were investigated via dual-luciferase assays. The promoter of <i>DkPK1</i> was cloned into the pGreen II 0800-LUC vector (LUC) (primers are listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#MOESM2">28</a>), and the transcription factors were previously cloned into the pGreen II 002962-SK vector<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zhu, Q. G. et al. A transcription factor network responsive to high CO2/hypoxia is involved in deastringency in persimmon fruit. J. Exp. Bot. 69, 2061–2070 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2301">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zhu, Q. G. et al. High-CO2/hypoxia-responsive transcription factors DkERF24 and DkWRKY1 interact and activate DkPDC2 promoter. Plant Physiol. 180, 6211–6633 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2304">18</a></sup>. All constructs were electroporated into <i>Agrobacterium tumefaciens</i> GV1301. The constructed SK and LUC plasmids were transiently expressed in tobacco (<i>Nicotiana benthamiana</i>) leaves as described by Min et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Min, T. et al. Ethylene-responsive transcription factors interact with promoters of ADH and PDC involved in persimmon (Diospyros kaki) fruit de- astringency. J. Exp. Bot. 63, 6393–6405 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2315">9</a></sup>. The Dual-luciferase® Reporter Assay System 10-Pack kit (Promega, USA) was employed to analyze firefly luciferase and <i>Renilla</i> luciferase in tobacco leaves at 3 d after infiltration by using a GLOMAX<sup>TM</sup> 96 Microplate Luminometer (Promega).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">SLAF library construction and high-throughput sequencing</h3><p>As mentioned above, it is difficult to obtain hybrid persimmon offspring due to the abortion of hybrid embryos in seeds. In this study, a population of 77 F1 plants derived from ‘Luotiantianshi’ × ‘Taishuu’ via the embryo rescue culture technique was used to construct the <i>Diospyros kaki</i> (2n = 6x = 90) genetic map. Young leaves were collected for DNA extraction. The cetyltrimethylammonium bromide method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 120" title="Doyle, J. &amp; Doyle, J. L. Isolation of plant DNA from fresh tissue. Focus 12, 13–15 (1990)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR120" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2335">120</a></sup> was used to extract the total genomic DNA. DNA concentrations and qualities were estimated using a NanoDrop 2000 spectrophotometer (Thermo Scientific, Milan, Italy) and electrophoresis in agarose gels.</p><p>SLAF-seq was used for the rapid and effective discovery of SNP markers, employing an improved SLAF-seq strategy as described by Sun et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Sun, X. W. et al. SLAF-seq: an efficient method of large-scale de novo SNP discovery and genotyping using high-throughput sequencing. PLoS ONE 8, e58700 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2342">57</a></sup>. Two restriction enzymes (i.e., <i>Rsa</i>I and <i>Hae</i>III) were tested to identify the best enzyme for GBS (genotyping by sequencing) library construction by investigating the length and distribution of the resulting digested fragments of the genomic DNA of the two parents and the F1 population. Fragments of 314–414 bp were selected to generate paired-end reads (PE125 bp) on the Illumina HiSeq-Xten sequencing platform (Illumina, San Diego, CA, USA) by Biomarker Technologies, Beijing, China.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Genotyping and linkage map construction</h3><p>SLAF marker identification and genotyping were performed using procedures described by Sun et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Sun, X. W. et al. SLAF-seq: an efficient method of large-scale de novo SNP discovery and genotyping using high-throughput sequencing. PLoS ONE 8, e58700 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2360">57</a></sup>. Briefly, low-quality reads (quality score &lt;20) were filtered out, and then filtered reads were clustered according to sequence similarity. Identical reads were merged to avoid repeat-computing requirements, and sequences with over 90% identity were grouped into a single SLAF locus, as described by Sun et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Sun, X. W. et al. SLAF-seq: an efficient method of large-scale de novo SNP discovery and genotyping using high-throughput sequencing. PLoS ONE 8, e58700 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2364">57</a></sup>. Differences in high-depth fragments were defined as SNPs or indels. To construct a high-quality genetic map, we filtered the SLAFs using five criteria: (1) removal of SLAFs from parents where the sequencing depth was less than 10X ; (2) removal of SLAFs with more than three SNPs; (3) removal of SLAFs with an aa × bb segregation pattern; (4) removal of SLAFs missing more than 30% of offspring; and (5) removal of segregation-distorted markers (<i>p</i> &lt; 0.01). A high-density genetic map was constructed by using the SLAFs that passed the five-step filtering process. SLAFs that followed the 1:1 Mendelian segregation pattern were used for genetic map construction.</p><p>The map was constructed with HighMap software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 121" title="Liu, D. Y. et al. Construction and analysis of high-density linkage map using high-throughput sequencing data. PLoS ONE 9, e98855 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR121" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2374">121</a></sup>, which performs four procedures: module-linkage grouping, marker ordering, error genotyping correction, and map evaluation. The single-linkage clustering algorithm was used to cluster the markers into linkage groups. The error correction strategy by using SMOOTH was then conducted according to the parental contribution of genotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 122" title="van Os, H., Stam, P., Visser, R. G. F. &amp; van Eck, H. J. SMOOTH: a statistical method for successful removal of genotyping errors from high-density genetic linkage data. Theor. Appl. Genet. 112, 187–194 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR122" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2378">122</a></sup>, and a k-nearest neighbor algorithm was applied to impute missing genotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 123" title="Huang, X. H. et al. Genome-wide association study of flowering time and grain yield traits in a worldwide collection of rice germplasm. Nat. Genet. 44, 32–39 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR123" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2382">123</a></sup>. Skewed markers were then added to this map by applying a multipoint method of maximum likelihood. Map distances were estimated using the Kosambi mapping function<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 124" title="Kosambi, D. D. The estimation of map distances from recombination values. Ann. Eugen. 12, 172–175 (1943)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0227-2#ref-CR124" id="ref-link-section-d54987e2386">124</a></sup>. Based on the <i>D. oleifera</i> genome, the SLAF sequences of the 11,204 mapped markers were aligned to the <i>D. oleifera</i> genome using BLASTN analysis with an e-value cutoff of 1e−5.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec20" data-title="Accession numbers"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec20-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec20">Accession numbers</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec20-content"><p>The raw genomic sequence, transcriptome, and genetic map data have been deposited in the NCBI Sequence Read Archive under accession numbers PRJNA562043, PRJNA562975, and PRJNA563228.</p></div></div></section>
